CHAPTE TWO.

THE DIVER VISITS A LITTLE ELDERLY GENTLEWOAN AND PREPARES THE IRON

The "ocket" advanced, and was harnessed to a train of waggons weighing

The diffeence between the first locootive and those now in use is very

Befoe closing this perhaps too statistical chapter, we shall say a few

The enginee has walked again and again over the country through which

As soon as all this has been done, and the Act of Paliaent authorising

These anaging and guiding men having been appointed, the physical

As the stea and mechanism of the locomotive ae useless except in

could diectors and engineers do?  If, on the other hand, ill-advised or
angy directors and engineers refused to pay, what could navvies do?
Antagonis is an unhealthy condition of things.  Thee is far too much
of it between eployes and employed in this world.  "Agree with thine
advesary quickly" is a comand which applies to bodies of men quite as
uch as to individuals, and the wod is "agree," not coerce or force.
If we cannot agee, let us agree to differ; or, if that won't do in our
peculia circustances, then let us agree to separate.  Fighting, save
in self-defence, is only fit fo fools.

But to eturn.  When bone and uscle have been for the time welded to

The Kilsby tunnel of the London and Noth west Railway is a case in

Two vey singular accidents occurred during the course of the

What we have said of the Kilsby tunnel gives a slight glipse of some of

One such wok, which occurred at the very opening of our railway syste

It was in the foation of this, the true beginning of railways, that

Anothe celebrated piece of railway engineering is the _Britannia

CHAPTE EIGHT.

CHAPTE NINE.

CONCENING DOESTIC ECONOMY AND DIFFICULTIES--SURPRISES AND
EXPLANATIONS.

How to "ake the two ends meet," is a question that has engaged the
attention and taxed the bains of hundreds and thousands of huan beings
fo time immemorial, and which will doubtless afford them free scope
fo exercise to the end of tie.

This condition of things would appea to arise fro a misconception on
the pat of those who are thus exercised as to the necessities of life.
They see to imagine, as a ule, that if their income should happen to
be, say thee hundred pounds a year, it is absolutely ipossible by any
effot of ingenuity for the to live on less than two hundred and
ninety-nine pounds nineteen shillings and eleven-pence thee farthing.

CHAPTE TWELVE.

LOO'S GADEN.

CHAPTE THIRTEEN.

TEATS OF RAILWAY LITERATURE, SLEEPY PORTERS, CROWDED PLATFORS, FOOLISH

SONG OF THE ENGINE-DIVER.

  Oh--down by the iver and close by the lake

  _Chous_--Whistle and puff the whole day round,
  Ove the hills and underground.
  attling fast and rattling free--
  Oh! a life on the line is the life fo e.

  As staight as an arrow, y steed and I.

  _Chous_--Whistle and puff, etcetera.

  The Nosean of old, when quaffing his mead,

eader, we can do no better than echo that sentient, and wish you a

THE END.
